Late Time Behavior of Non Spherical Collapse
of Scalar Field Dark Matter
Argelia Bernal1,2 and F. Siddhartha GuzmÂ´an2
1 Departmento de FÂ´Ä±sica, Centro De InvestigaciÂ´on y De Estudios Avanzados Del
IPN, AP 14-740,07000 MÂ´exico D.F., MÂ´exico <abernalresca@fis.cinvestav.mx>
2 Instituto de FÂ´Ä±sica y MatemÂ´aticas, Universidad Michoacana de San NicolÂ´as de
Hidalgo. Ediï¬cio C-3, Cd. Universitaria, C.P.58040 Morelia, MichoacÂ´an, MÂ´exico.
<guzman@ifm.umich.mx>
We show for the ï¬rst time the evolution of non-spherically symmetric balls of a
self-gravitating scalar ï¬eld in the Newtonian regime. In order to do so, we use
a ï¬nite diï¬€erencing approximation of the ShcrÂ¨odinger-Poisson (SP) system of
equations with axial symmetry in cylindrical coordinates. Our results indicate
that spherically symmetric equilibrium conï¬gurations of the SP system are
late-time attractors for non-spherically symmetric initial proï¬les of the scalar
ï¬eld, which is a generalization of such behavior for spherically symmetric
initial proï¬les. Our system and the boundary conditions used, work as a model
of scalar ï¬eld dark matter collapse after the turnaround point. In such case, we
have found that spherically symmetric halos are late time attractor solutions
of possible axisymmetric initial scalar ï¬eld overdensities.
1 Introduction
Recently, scalar ï¬elds have played the role in several scenarios related to as-
trophysical phenomena. The reason is that such ï¬elds are quite common in
theoretical physics, specially branches related to theories beyond the standard
model of particles, higher dimensional theories and brane world models of the
universe. In the present research we deal with the scalar ï¬eld dark matter
model (SFDM), which assumes the dark matter to be a classical minimally
coupled real scalar ï¬eld determined by a cosh-like potential. Such potential
provides the ï¬eld with the necessary properties to mimic the behavior and
successes of cold dark matter at cosmic scales. In fact in [1, 2, 3] it was shown
that the mass parameter of the scalar ï¬eld gets ï¬xed by a desired cut-oï¬€of
the power spectrum, which has two eï¬€ects: i) the theory gets ï¬xed and ii)
there is no overabundance of substructure, which standard cold dark matter
cannot achieve. One important consequence is that the boson has to be ultra-
light with masses around m âˆ¼10âˆ’21,âˆ’23eV. This is a substantially important
271
A. CarramiËœnana et al. (eds.), Solar, Stellar and Galactic Connections between Particle
Physics and Astrophysics, 271â€“278.
câƒ2007 Springer.

272
Argelia Bernal and F. Siddhartha GuzmÂ´an
bound, because in the standard dark matter models there are no such ultra-
light dark matter candidates. The beneï¬t obtained however, is two fold: the
scalar ï¬eld can represent a Bose Condensate of such ultralight particles and
the de Broglie wavelength forbids the scalar ï¬eld to form cuspy structures.
After the ï¬‚uctuation analysis about this candidate and its correspond-
ing concordance with observations -the model mimics the properties of the
Î›CDM at cosmic scales-, the next step has to be in the direction of the study
of structure formation and the explanation of local phenomena, like rotation
curves in galaxies. Fortunately there have been important advances in such
direction [4, 5]. In [6] it was shown that relativistic self-gravitating scalar
ï¬eld conï¬gurations can be formed when they have galactic masses provided
the mass of the boson is ultralight. Nevertheless, because the gravitational
ï¬eld in galaxies is weak, the race turned into the newtonian limit of the sys-
tem of equations, which was developed in [7]. The price to be paid is that
it is not possible to apply the approach at very early stages of the evolution
of the universe, and the proï¬t is that the scalar ï¬eld in the non-relativistic
regime provides a clear interpretation within the Bose Condensate formal-
ism and classical quantum mechanics. In both cases, the strong gravity and
the Newtonian regimes, a wide range of arbitrary spherically symmetric ini-
tial conï¬gurations collapse and form gravitationally bounded and virialized
objects with a smooth density everywhere (except those that are related to
unstable initial conï¬gurations that collapse into black holes in the strong ï¬eld
regime) [8, 9]. This property seems to be fundamental in order to form galactic
halos, because several high resolution observations are consistent with regular
galactic dark matter proï¬les in the center of the galaxies [10, 11, 12, 13].
It is clear then, that two approaches are in progress, both are comple-
mentary and are useful to explore the SFDM hypothesis. Turning back to
the astrophysical scenario, at stages after the turnaround point where weak
ï¬eld applies, the question is whether dark matter halos are gravitationally
bounded objects of scalar ï¬eld which have been formed through a gravita-
tional collapse of initial scalar ï¬eld overdensities. The newtonian version of
the Einstein-Klein-Gordon system of equations is provided by the SchrÂ¨odinger-
Poisson equations (SP), which are the ones that lead the gravitational collapse
of the system. This approximation should work for the evolution of an initial
density proï¬le after the epoch when the overdensity ï¬‚uctuation starts to evolve
independently of the cosmological expansion.
In the recent past, it has been found that in spherical symmetry the SP
system has stationary solutions, from which only the nodeless one is stable
[8, 14]; even further, it has been found that such conï¬gurations behave as
late-time attractors for initially quite arbitrary spherically symmetric den-
sity proï¬les [7, 15]. The goal of the present manuscript is to show that such
attractor behavior extends beyond spherical symmetry, still within axially
symmetric initial density proï¬les.
In the next section we present the code to solve the axially symmetric
SP system and enumerate the physical quantities that characterize the axial

conï¬gurations. In order to provide testbeds of our code, in section 2 we show
the evolution of spherically symmetric equilibrium conï¬gurations, which be-
havior is well known; latter in the same section we show the evolution of
non equilibrium axial symmetric conï¬gurations and the attractor behavior of
the spherically symmetric ground states. Finally in section 3 we draw some
comments and conclusions.

Another important issue has to do with the boundary conditions. We as-

being the wave equation and no sommerfeld boundary conditions can be ap-
plied as far as we are aware. However we emulate open boundaries by applying
fully reï¬‚ecting boundary conditions and implementing a sponge region in the
outermost grid points of the domain. The sponge consists in adding in such a
region an imaginary potential to the SchrÂ¨odinger equation, whose eï¬€ects are
those of a sink of density of probability. Thus the particles near the boundaries
are trapped by the sponge. Details about the implementation of the sponge
for spherical symmetry can be found in [8], and we just implemented the same
idea in a two dimensional domain.

2.1 Tests of the Code
As a testbed for the code, it is necessary to verify whether in these non-
spherical coordinates the code is able to reproduce the expected results for
an equilibrium conï¬guration, that is, a spherically symmetric stationary solu-
tion. In spherical symmetry, in order to construct stationary conï¬gurations the
scalar ï¬eld is assumed to have the form Î¦ = eâˆ’iÏƒtÏ†(x); provided the bound-
ary conditions Ï† = 0 at inï¬nity and demanding the gravitational potential to
be smooth at the origin, the SP system becomes an eigenvalue problem. The
eigenvalues Ïƒ related to the eigenfunctions Î¦ are non degenerate and take a
diï¬€erent values for diï¬€erent central ï¬eld Ï†(0). Because the time dependence
of Î¦ is harmonic, Ï = Î¦Î¦âˆ—and therefore the gravitational potential U should
be time independent. Another property of the equilibrium conï¬gurations con-
structed in this way is the number of nodes of the wave function; the solution
with a nodeless wave function is said to be in the ground state and it has been
shown (see [8]) that only the ground state solutions are stable. In fact a linear
perturbation analysis has revealed that such ground state systems oscillate
with a speciï¬c angular frequency when slightly perturbed, a property that
is quite useful when dealing with the discretized version of the SP equations.
Another important property of the system (1) is the following scale invariance:

system- at Î³ âˆ¼0.046 (see text for details). These plots, together with the convergence
of the virial relation indicate that our code works properly.
2.2 Evolution of Non Spherical Initial Proï¬les
In this section we show that spherically symmetric ground states, are late
time attractors for initial conï¬gurations which are not spherically symmetric.

276
Argelia Bernal and F. Siddhartha GuzmÂ´an
A ï¬rst step would be to show that equilibrium conï¬gurations are stable against
axisymmetric perturbations, and a second step would be to show how quite
arbitrary axisymmetric initial data evolve toward a spherical equilibrium con-
ï¬guration. We decided to skip the ï¬rst step and go straight to the second one.
We carried out a series of simulations with initial data consisting of the su-
perposition of an equilibrium conï¬guration with Ï†(0) = 1 and a gaussian-like
proï¬le given by the expression Î´Ïˆ = Ae[âˆ’x2/Ïƒ2
xâˆ’z2/Ïƒ2
z] that we call invading
proï¬le. The parameters of the invading proï¬le for the cases shown here corre-
spond to Ïƒx = 1 and Ïƒz = 1.5, with amplitudes A = 0.1, 0.2, which implies an
addition of 5% and 9% of the total mass of the original equilibrium conï¬gu-
ration. The reason to proceed in such way is that beyond the perturbation of
the system we wonder whether the invading particles attach to the spherical
conï¬guration and together they evolve toward a rescaled equilibrium conï¬g-
uration.
The result is that the whole system evolves toward a stationary conï¬gura-
tion through the gravitational cooling process [8, 15], which is powered by the
ejection of a small amount of scalar ï¬eld mass of 0.05% and 0.12% respectively.
In the left panel of Figure 2, we show the evolution of the mass M versus the
central density Ïc = Ï(x = 0, z = 0) for both initial proï¬les. The solid line
is the branch of all the equilibrium conï¬gurations constructed as described
above. What can be observed in the plot is that the initial conï¬gurations
oscillate around the branch of equilibrium conï¬gurations and at late times it
tends to converge to one of such solutions. This attractor behavior has been
shown for spherically symmetric conï¬gurations in [15] and is shown here for
the ï¬rst time for the case of non-spherical proï¬les. However, the information
related to the convergence to an equilibrium conï¬guration is not enough, and
in the right panel of Figure 2 we show the ellipticity of the system, which
we deï¬ne as the integrated diï¬€erence between Ïz = Ï(0, z) and Ïx = Ï(x, 0)
measured from the center of mass of the conï¬guration; we can see that after
a transient with initial ellipticity the system relaxes and becomes spherical in
the continuum limit.
3 Conclusions
We have presented a new code that solves the SP system of equations with
axial symmetry using ï¬nite diï¬€erencing and an explicit time integrator. We
tested our code with the evolution of an equilibrium spherically symmetric
ground state conï¬guration, and obtained the correct values of the fundamental
frequency of perturbations due to the discretization errors; in fact we showed
that such error is spherical and no axisymmetric modes are excited through
the discretization of the SP equations in cylindrical coordinates. Moreover we
veriï¬ed that the virial state of such system behaves in the expected way in
the continuum limit.

Using such code we evolved non-spherically symmetric conï¬gurations made
with the superposition of an equilibrium conï¬guration and a gaussian-like ax-

In the context of the scalar ï¬eld dark matter model we have quite a new

proï¬les, and moreover, initially axisymmetric proï¬les tend toward a spherical
ground state.

Inhomogeneous Dark Matter in Non-trivial
Interaction with Dark Energy
Roberto A. Sussman1, Israel Quiros2 and Osmel MartÂ´Ä±n GonzÂ´alez2
1 Instituto de Ciencias Nucleares, Apartado Postal 70543, UNAM, MÂ´exico DF,
04510, MÂ´exico <sussman@nuclecu.unam.mx>.
2 Departamento de FÂ´Ä±sica, Universidad Central de las Villas, Santa Clara, Cuba
<israel@uclv.edu.cu; osmel@uclv.edu.cu>.
We study interacting dark energy (DE) and cold dark matter (DM) in the con-
text of an inhomogeneous and anisotropic spacetime. DM and DE are modeled
as an interactive mixture of inhomogeneous dust (DM) and a generic homoge-
neous dark energy (DE) ï¬‚uid. By choosing an â€œequation of stateâ€ linking the
energy density (Âµ) and pressure (p) of the DE ï¬‚uid, as well as a free function
governing the radial dependence, the models become fully determinate and can
be applied to known speciï¬c DE sources, such as quintessense scalar ï¬elds or
tachyonic ï¬‚uids. For the case of the simple equation of state p = (Î³ âˆ’1) Âµ
with 0 â‰¤Î³ < 2/3, the free parameters and boundary conditions can be se-
lected for an adequate description of a local DM overdensity evolving in a
suitable cosmic background that accurately ï¬ts current observational data. If
the particular case when DE ï¬‚uid corresponds to a quintessense scalar ï¬eld,
the interaction term can be associated with a well motivated nonâ€“minimal
coupling to the DM component. The eï¬€ects of inhomogeneity and anisotropy
yield diï¬€erent local behavior and evolution rates for observational parameters
in the local overdense region.
1 Introduction
Observational data on Type Ia supernovae strongly suggests that the universe
is expanding at an accelerated rate [1, 2]. This eï¬€ect has lead to the wide-
spread assumption that the inventory of cosmic matterâ€“energy could contain,
besides baryons, photons, neutrinos and cold dark matter (DM)3, an extra
contribution generically known as â€œdark energyâ€ (DE), whose kinematic ef-
fect could be equivalent to that of a ï¬‚uid with negative pressure. While the
large scale dynamics of the main cosmic sources (DE and DM) is more or
3 We shall assume henceforth that DM is of the â€œcoldâ€ variety, i.e. CDM.
279
A. CarramiËœnana et al. (eds.), Solar, Stellar and Galactic Connections between Particle
Physics and Astrophysics, 279â€“294.
câƒ2007 Springer.

280
Roberto A. Sussman, Israel Quiros and Osmel MartÂ´Ä±n GonzÂ´alez
less understood, their fundamental physical nature is still a matter for de-
bate, thus various physical explanations have been suggested. Cold DM is
usually conceived as a collisionless gas of supersymmetric particles (neutrali-
nos), while DE can be modeled as a â€œcosmological constantâ€, quintessense
scalar ï¬elds, tachyonic ï¬‚uids, generalized forces, etc [3, 4]. The standard ap-
proach is mostly to consider a Friedman-LemaË†Ä±tre-Robertson-Walker (FLRW)
metric, with linear perturbations, making also the simplest assumption that
DE only interacts gravitationally with DM. However, there are still some un-
resolved issues, such as the soâ€“called â€œcoincidence problemâ€, concerning the
odd apparent fact that the critical densities of DM and DE approximately
coincide in our cosmic era [5, 6]. Aiming at a solution to this problem and
bearing in mind our ignorance on the fundamental physics of DM and DE,
various models have been proposed recently which include assorted forms of
interaction between these sources [7, 8, 9, 10, 11].
It is customarily assumed that DE dominates large scale cosmic dynamics,
so that DM inhomogeneities in galactic clusters and superclusters can be
considered a local eï¬€ect or can be treated by means of linear perturbations
in a FLRW background. Thus, a reasonable generalization of existing models
could be to assume inhomogeneous DM interacting with homogeneous DE,
so that large scale dynamics is governed by the latter. We propose in this
paper a class of analytic models which provide a reasonable description of
inhomogeneous DM interacting with a generic homogeneous DE source. The
models are based on the decomposition of a perfect ï¬‚uid tensor as a mixture
of an inhomogeneous dust component (DM) plus a homogeneous perfect ï¬‚uid
with negative pressure (generic DE), as the matter source of the spherically
symmetric subcase of the Szafronâ€“Szekeres exact solutions of Einsteinâ€™s ï¬eld
equations [12]. However, the underlying geometry of the models we present
can be easily generalized to include nonâ€“spherical symmetries or even the case
without any isometry, since Szafronâ€“Szekeres solutions do not usually admit
Killing vectors.
The decomposition of a perfect ï¬‚uid tensor as a mixture of an inhomo-
geneous dust component plus a homogeneous ï¬‚uid has been considered pre-
viously [13, 14] but in the context of mixtures of baryons and radiation. We
consider in this paper only the type of models examined in [14], by assum-
ing the homogeneous ï¬‚uid to describe a generic DE source, while the dust
component corresponds to inhomogeneous DM, all of which is a reasonable
assumption since the dynamical eï¬€ects of quintessence mostly become domi-
nant in very large scales, larger than the â€œhomogeneity scaleâ€ (100â€“300 Mpc),
while DM (galactic clusters and superclusters) is very inhomogeneous at scales
of this magnitude and smaller.
In order to determine the time evolution of the sources, we need to as-
sume a physical model, or â€œequation of stateâ€ for the generic DE source (the
homogeneous ï¬‚uid). Thus, we assume in section VIII a simple â€œgamma lawâ€
equation of state of the form p = (Î³ âˆ’1) Âµ, where p, Âµ are the pressure and
matterâ€“energy density of the DE source. Such an equation of state leads to a

Inhomogeneous Dark Matter in Non-trivial Interaction with Dark Energy
281
DE homogeneous ï¬‚uid evolving like a FLRW ï¬‚uid with ï¬‚at spacelike sections
with a scaling law of the form Âµ âˆtâˆ’2, which is compatible with a scalar ï¬eld
with an exponential potential [15]. Although this is a very simple type of DE
source, it yields analytic forms for the DM density, observational parameters
and all other relevant physical and geometric quantities.
The assumption of a gamma law equation of state fully determines the
time dependence of all relevant quantities, while the spacial dependence is
determined once we select an arbitrary function whose form depends on the
choice of suitable boundary conditions associated with a description of a local
DM overdense region in a DE dominated cosmic background that accurately
complies with observational constraints on observational parameters: â„¦for
DM and for DE and the deceleration parameter q. We provide a full graphical
illustration of the interplay between â€œlocalâ€ and â€œcosmic backgroundâ€ eï¬€ects
on these observational parameters: for example, anisotropy emerges in the
local dependence of these quantities on the â€œoï¬€-center observation angleâ€ Ïˆ
(Figure 2), while inhomogeneity leads to local conditions in the overdense
region (DM dominates over DE and q is positive) that are diï¬€erent from
those of the cosmic background: DE dominates and q < 0, as required by an
â€œacceleratedâ€ universe whose large scale dynamics is dominated by a repulsive
force associated with DE (Figures 3).
The issue of the interaction between DE and DM is dealt with in section
VI. We show that the individual momentumâ€“energy tensors for DM and DE
are not independently conserved, thus the models are incompatible with these
components interacting only gravitationally. However, if we assume the DE
ï¬‚uid to be a scalar ï¬eld quintessense type of source, then the models can
accommodate various prescriptions for a DEâ€“DM interaction, like those pro-
posed in the literature [7, 8, 9, 10, 11]. Finally, in section VII we present a
discussion and summary of our results. The results presented in here have
been further generalized and expanded in [16].

6 Interaction Between the Mixture Components
As we mentioned before, the two mixture components: DM (inhomogeneous
dust) plus DE (homogeneous ï¬‚uid) are not separately conserved. Considering
(9) and (10), the energy balance for the total energyâ€“momentum tensor, Ë™e +

Since each term in square brackets in the left hand side of (32) corresponds to

and DE remains so far unknown, we cannot rule out the existence of such
interaction. Notice that once a given model has been determined by specifying
an â€œequation of stateâ€ p = p(Âµ) and a form for f = f(r), as we have done
in the previous sections, this interaction term would also be fully determined.
In general, if the interaction term in (33-33) is a negative valued function,
then DM transfers energy into the DE and viceversa. Considering the free
parameters given by (19), (27) and (31), we plot in Figure 4 the interaction
term Q in (33-33), as a function of Ï„ and log10 r. Notice that Q is initially
positive and remains so today (DE transfers energy to DM at Ï„ = Ï„0) but
will change sign in a future time (DM transfers energy to DE), tending to
zero asymptotically as Ï„ â†’âˆ. The timeâ€“asymptotic state is that of only
gravitational interaction between DE and DM (i.e. separate conservation of
each component).
However, the relevant question is not so much the explicit functional form
of Q, but its interpretation in terms of a selfâ€“consistent physical theory that
would be regulating the interaction between DM and DE. In fact, one of the
challenges of modern cosmology is to propose such a selfâ€“consistent theoretical
model of this interaction, while agreeing at the same time with the experimen-
tal and observational data. In this context, the interaction between DM and
DE has been considered, using homogeneous FLRW cosmologies, in trying
to understand the soâ€“called â€œcoincidence problemâ€, that is, the suspiciously
coincidental fact the DE and DM energy densities are of the same order of
magnitude in our present cosmic era [5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 11].

7 Conclusion
We have presented a class of inhomogeneous cosmological models whose source
is an interacting mixture of DM (dust) and a generic DE ï¬‚uid. The relevance
of the present paper emerges when we realize that there are surprisingly few
studies in which DE and DM are the sources of inhomogeneous and anisotropic
spacetimes (see [22]), as practically all study of the dynamical evolution of
these components is carried on in the context of homogeneous and isotropic
FLRW cosmologies or linear perturbations on a FLRW background. There
are also very few papers that examine the possibility of nonâ€“gravitational
interaction between DE and DM.
Once we assume or prescribe an â€œequation of stateâ€ (i.e. a relation between
pressure, p, and matterâ€“energy density, Âµ, of the DE ï¬‚uid), we have a speciï¬c
DE model (quintessense scalar ï¬elds, tachyonic ï¬‚uid, etc) and all the timeâ€“
dependent parameters can be determined by solving diï¬€erential equations
reminiscent of FLRW ï¬‚uids. Since the spatial dependence of all quantities
is governed by the function f = f(r), once the latter is selected the mod-
els become fully determined. In order to work out this process we chose the
simple â€œgamma lawâ€ equation of state: p = (Î³ âˆ’1)Âµ (equation (19)), leading
to analytic forms for all relevant quantities, including the main observational
parameters, â„¦DE, â„¦DM and q. Our choice for a DE ï¬‚uid complying with (19)
is equivalent to a scalar ï¬eld with exponential potential, satisfying a scaling
power law dependence on t [15]. Although this is a very idealized quintessense
model, our aim has been to use it as a guideline to illustrate how more so-
phisticated DE scenarios can be incorporated in future work involving the
models.
As shown in Figures 3, the models homogenize and isotropize asymptoti-
cally in cosmic time for all fundamental observers and/or assumptions on the
DE ï¬‚uid, thus they are well suited for studying the interaction between DE
and DM in the context of the evolution of large scale inhomogeneities (of the
order of the scale of homogeneity âˆ¼100 âˆ’300 Mpc). By selecting appropriate

Inhomogeneous Dark Matter in Non-trivial Interaction with Dark Energy
293
boundary conditions (form of f), we can examine inhomogeneities at various
scales and/or asymptotic conditions (see Figures 2 and 3). In particular, we
have explored the case of a local DM overdense region, whose scale can be ar-
bitrarily ï¬xed and with an asymptotic behavior that accurately converges to
a cosmic background characterized by observational parameters that ï¬t cur-
rently accepted observational constraints: 0.6 < â„¦DE < 0.8, 0.2 < â„¦DM < 0.4
and q â‰ˆâˆ’0.5 (see Figure 4). As illustrated by the various graphical examples
that we have presented, this interplay between a local overdensity, a cosmic
background and a transition region between them, shows in a spectacular
manner how inhomogeneity and anisotropy lead to interesting and important
information that cannot be appreciated in models based on FLRW metrics
and/or linear perturbations. For example, as revealed by Figure 2, the eï¬€ect
of anisotropy emerges as a dependence of observational parameters on local
observation angles in â€œoï¬€center positionsâ€, an eï¬€ect which is only signiï¬cant
in the transition between the overdensity and the cosmic background. On the
other hand, as shown by Figures 3, inhomogeneity allows for radically diï¬€er-
ent ratios between DM and DE in the overdensity and the cosmic background,
so that DM dominates over DE, locally, in the overdense region, as a contrast
with DE dominating DM, asymptotically, in the cosmic background (as ex-
pected). Also, while q is negative in the cosmic (DE dominated) background,
thus denoting the expected â€œrepulsiveâ€ accelerated expansion at large scales,
we have q > 0 along smaller scales in the local overdensity. For all parameters
there is a smooth convergence between local and asymptotic values in the
transition region.
We have also examined the nonâ€“gravitational interaction between DE and
DM. Plotting the interaction term, Q, (Figure 4) shows that energy ï¬‚ows from
DE to DM at the present cosmic era, with the ï¬‚ow reversing direction in the
future and evolving towards an asymptotic future state characterized by pure
gravitational interaction: Q â†’0. If we take the DE ï¬‚uid to be a quintessense
scalar ï¬eld, the DE vs DM interaction can be incorporated to the theoretical
framework of an action like (35), associated with a nonâ€“minimal coupling of
scalar ï¬elds and DM. Since DM is inhomogeneous while the scalar ï¬eld is
homogeneous, only for some particular forms of spacial dependence (i.e. the
function f) we obtain a coupling function expressible as Ï‡ = Ï‡(Ï†). In gen-
eral, we have to allow for the possibility that Ï‡ = Ï‡(Ï†(t), r). However, we
have examined this interaction just in qualitative terms, with the purpose of
illustrating the methodology to follow in future applications. As guidelines for
future work, we have the application of the models to more sophisticated and
better motivated DE formalisms, perhaps in the context of the â€œcoincidenceâ€
problem [5, 6, 7, 8, 9, 10, 11]. See [16] for a more comprehensive version of
this article.

L. Arturo UreËœnaâ€“LÂ´opez1

Our aim in this review is to brieï¬‚y describe the main results in the topic of
scalar ï¬eld dark matter, in which a scalar ï¬eld is the dark matter particle.
We will start with a revision of the concept of scalar ï¬elds at the classical
and quantum levels. We will then continue with some of the relevant solu-
tions for self-gravitating scalar ï¬elds in a cosmological setting. At the end, we
will discuss the constraints cosmological observations impose upon the free
parameters of a scalar ï¬eld dark matter model.
1 What is a Scalar Field?

1.1 Quantization of a Free Scalar Field
The accepted interpretation of scalar ï¬elds is that their excitations, once we
quantize them, represent particle states so that the single-particle interpreta-
tion of the wave function Ï† should be abandoned. In this section, we brieï¬‚y
describe the quantization of scalar ï¬elds, (for details see [1, 2, 3]).
To begin with, we consider the (ï¬‚at) Minkowski spacetime in which gÂµÎ½ =

a massive particle, and then the general solution is given as a superposition
of (properly normalized) plane waves of the form Ï†p(t, x) âˆeipÂ·xâˆ’iEt, with
momentum p and energy E2 = p2 +m2 (p = |p|). This solution reinforces the
interpretation of parameter m as the mass of the scalar ï¬eld.
At the quantum level, the scalar ï¬eld Ï† is taken as a quantum operator, so

1.2 Self-gravitating Scalar Fields
All of the above formalism can be generalized to the case of an arbitrary
curved spacetime with metric gÂµÎ½. We would like to stress in here that ex-
pansion (3) can be used in any spacetime, curved or not. Hence, functions Ï†p
are (appropriately) orthonormal functions that form a complete basis of solu-
tions of the KG equation in a curved spacetime. The rest of the formalism is
preserved: assuming the existence of a vacuum state, a set of quantum states
can be constructed using the creation and annihilation operators3.
The gravitational interaction for classical scalar ï¬elds is taken into ac-
count by the Einstein equations, which are generically written in the form
GÂµÎ½ = 8Ï€ GTÂµÎ½. The so called Einstein tensor GÂµÎ½ is constructed from the
geometrical properties of the spacetime, while TÂµÎ½ is the energy-momentum
tensor of matter. For a classical scalar ï¬eld, the energy-momentum tensor can
be obtained from the Lagrangian (1), and then

appears on the r.h.s. of the Einstein equations; this is known as semiclassical
approximation that have revealed so many surprises.

When working with scalar ï¬elds, whether classical or quantum, we require
to solve the classical EKG equations in a curved spacetime. Hence, in the fol-

in Cosmology.
2 Cosmological Solutions
For a homogeneous and isotropic universe, the spacetime is described by the
Friedmann-Robertson-Walker metric. For simplicity, we shall only consider the
spatially ï¬‚at case. In such a space time, the scalar ï¬eld is homogeneous and

isotropic too, and then the (basis) functions depend only on time, Ï† = Ï†(t).
The KG equation we have to solve is
Â¨Ï† + 3H Ë™Ï† + m2Ï† = 0 ,
(9)

Eq. (9) can be solved easily if there is a regime in which H < m. In this
case, the scalar ï¬eld Ï† oscillates with a time scale (given by mâˆ’1) shorter
than the expansion time scale (given by Hâˆ’1), so that the universe only feels
the energy density averaged on a large number of oscillations. The averaged
energy density goes as âŸ¨ÏÏ†âŸ©âˆ¼aâˆ’3, and then the scalar ï¬eld behaves as cold
dark matter [4, 5, 6]. This is the main result of this section.
The discussion in the above paragraph is quite general, and applies to any
massive scalar ï¬eld, that is, to any scalar ï¬eld potential with a minimum
around which it can be written quadratically in ï¬rst approximation.
3 (Real) Scalar Field Stars: Oscillatons
We are also interested in studying the kind of gravitationally-bounded objects
scalar ï¬elds can form. The motivation for this is to investigate whether a scalar
ï¬eld, which in some circumstances can behave as cold dark matter, can also
form stable cosmological structure.
It is known that there are long-lived solutions of the EKG equations in a
spherically symmetric spacetime, which are generically called oscillatons [7].
We will describe below these objects in both the relativistic and Newtonian
regimes.
3.1 Relativistic Oscillatons
The most general spherically-symmetric spacetime in the polar-areal slicing
reads

that the most general solution can be given as a (convergent) Fourier series
of the form [7, 8]

3.2 Newtonian Oscillatons
The relativistic conï¬gurations described above are in the strong-ï¬eld regime,
and can be very compact objects. However, it is also our interest to study the
weak-ï¬eld limit, in which gravity interactions and velocities (v/c â‰ª1) are
small [9, 10].

of the SN system, the frequency should scale as Î³ â†’Î»2Î³.
Due to the scaling symmetry, all of the possible Newtonian oscillatons
can be obtained through a scale transformation applied to a properly chosen
equilibrium conï¬guration. For simplicity, one usually chooses the one in which
Ï•(0) = 1.

4 Final Discussion
We have left for this last section the discussion about the parameters of a
scalar ï¬eld model for dark matter. Up to this point, we have described the

properties of self-gravitating scalar ï¬elds endowed with a quadratic scalar
potential. We argued that such a potential is a good approximation to any
scalar potential with a minimum, and then the relevant free parameter is the
scalar ï¬eld mass m.
We have just to ï¬nd out which values of the scalar ï¬eld mass m could
have physically interesting consequences. The constraints we have to take
into account are the following.
â€¢
The standard cosmological model requires the existence of a matter domi-
nated era in order to allow the formation of structure. This in turn implies
that the scalar mass cannot be too small, and that m â‰¥Heq, where Heq
denotes the value of the Hubble parameter at the time of equivalence be-
tween radiation and matter [4, 6].
â€¢
On the other hand, real galaxies are Newtonian objects, for which v/c âˆ¼
10âˆ’3, so that Newtonian oscillatons should be the appropriate scalar ob-
jects to describe them, if we think of a scalar ï¬eld as a good dark matter
candidate. Hence, as the size of a Newtonian oscillaton would have to be
of the order (Î»m)âˆ’1 > 10 kpc, this implies that m â‰¤0.1pcâˆ’1 âˆ¼10âˆ’22eV
[10].
Resuming in, we see that the more massive a scalar ï¬eld is, the earlier that
it behaves as cold dark matter. On the other hand, the lighter a scalar ï¬eld
is, the larger a Newtonian oscillaton is, and then the more likely it can ï¬t
galactic observations.
Therefore, the two constraints above are complementary: cosmological evo-
lution imposes a lower bound on the scalar mass, whilst galactic evolution im-
poses on it an upper bound. That is, we cannot expect any scalar ï¬eld model
to ï¬t observations at all scales, but it must be a very particular model.

302
L. Arturo UreËœnaâ€“LÂ´opez
Our group has determined, through a more detailed study, that a massive
scalar ï¬eld should have a mass of about m âˆ¼10âˆ’23eV in order to accomplish
all of cosmological observations in a decent manner [4, 5].
However, there may be properties we are not taken into account properly,
as extra terms in the scalar potential may add desirable properties and then
alleviate the constraints imposed upon the free scalar ï¬eld. We expect to re-
port on them in a future communication.
